                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Navo3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_NaVO3">NaVO<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Solid-state+Method&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_solid-state%20method">solid-state method</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Kinetic+Study&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_kinetic%20study">kinetic study</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Diffusion+Coefficient&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20diffusion%20coefficient">sodium diffusion coefficient</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Amorphous-like&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_amorphous-like">amorphous-like</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have captivated significant interest, especially in the use of large-scale applications such as electrical energy storage systems. The advantages of sodium over lithium mainly include its low cost and worldwide abundant resources, which are the most important aspects to develop large-scale rechargeable batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> The practical implications of SIBs are facing challenges in designing high-performance electrode materials. Many of the electrode materials, which have already been investigated in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), can be transplanted in SIBs for the Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation. The positive electrode materials of SIBs are broadly investigated and have made great progress compared to their counterpart. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 6 to 10">(6−10)</a> The development of anode materials is more challenging for SIBs as commonly used anodes in LIBs such as graphite and high-capacity silicon does not de/intercalate sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Moreover, the anode materials in SIBs show poor cyclability because of the larger size of sodium ions (1.03 Å), which results in sluggish diffusion kinetics of sodium during reversible de/insertion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref12" aria-label="reference 1 and 12">(1,12)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Hard carbon is extensively studied in SIBs but it delivers limited reversible capacities between 100 and 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> Other carbonaceous materials such as graphene oxide, amorphous carbon, petroleum-coke carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> polyparaphenylene carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> soft carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and so forth deliver even lower capacities which are insufficient for use in practical applications. Several other types of anode materials are investigated with intercalation, conversion, alloying, and amorphous reaction mechanisms. Each has their own dis/advantages such as intercalated anode materials (Ti-based materials) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> reveal high structural stability but exhibit limited capacities of 100–200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, anode materials with conversion reaction (Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, NiCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS<sub>2</sub>, and Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 21 to 24">(21−24)</a> exhibit large capacities of 150–400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> but suffer with poor cyclability and alloying mechanism (Sn, Sb, and P), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a> and also show high capacities but suffer from poor cyclability because of the large volume expansion/contraction during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process. Thus, there is a need to find appropriate anode materials with high and stable capacities, which could be used for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Layered vanadium-based materials are known for multiple lithium intakes and exist with different compositions and oxidation states such as V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (5+), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> VO<sub>2</sub> (4+), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> LiVO<sub>3</sub> (5+), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> (multiple from 2+ to 5+), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> LiV<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> (5+), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> and NaVO<sub>3</sub> (5+). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> These materials are well-known to deliver large capacities in LIBs as cathode and anode materials based on multiple redox reactions of vanadium. Recently, NaVO<sub>3</sub> was synthesized by the sol–gel method and investigated as a high-performance anode where it delivered a capacity of 356 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> NaVO<sub>3</sub> is isostructural with CaMg(SiO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> which belongs to the pyroxene family where SiO<sub>3</sub> chains are held together by metal ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Similarly, VO<sub>3</sub> chains formed VO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra which are located at alternate channels with the NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedral in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Despite high capacities in LIBs and their structural advantages, vanadium-based layered materials have been less explored in SIBs. Further, the zero-oxidation state of vanadium was not found during the charge/discharge process because of the strong interaction of V–O bonds and thus do not form metallic V, suggesting a lesser volume variation compared to typical conversion-based electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, a layered NaVO<sub>3</sub> is synthesized using the simple solid-state route, and its electrochemical properties as an anode material for SIBs are investigated. A comprehensive kinetic study using different techniques is conducted to reveal the sodium diffusivity in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode. Furthermore, the sodium insertion/extraction mechanism was investigated to probe the structural changes using in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) techniques and the oxidation state of the material was determined using X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (&gt;99.0%) and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (&gt;99.6%) were purchased from Sigma and used without any further modification. NaVO<sub>3</sub> was synthesized by the solid-state process by mixing the stoichiometric ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. The mixture was well-ground with a mortar and pestle for ∼30 min and transferred to an alumina boat. The powder was heated at 600 °C for 6 h at a temperature ramp of 3 °C/min in the air in a box furnace. The obtained powder was analyzed with XRD (X-ray diffractometer from Rigaku equipped with radiations from a Cu source with a wavelength of 1.54 Å), field emission scanning electron microscopy (NanoSEM-FEI NOVA200), and high-resolution TEM (FEI Tecnai G2-F20). The X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) spectrum of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> was acquired with a PHI 5000 VersaProbe (ULVAC-PHI) under high vacuum conditions (6.8 × 10<sup>–8</sup> Pa). An Al Kα X-ray source (1486.6 eV) was used as a monochromatic to select the energy, and the carbon 1<i>s</i> peak (284.6 eV) was used for internal calibration. The peaks were fitted using the MultiPak software.</div><div class="NLM_p">Coin-type CR2032 cells were used to evaluate the electrochemical properties of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrodes. NaVO<sub>3</sub> powder, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene difluoride were mixed in a ratio of 7:2:1 to prepare the working electrodes. It is worthy to mention here that the mixing of NaVO<sub>3</sub> and acetylene black was done using the ball-milling (material/ball weight ratio was 1:20) process for 3 h in an argon atmosphere with a speed of 300 rpm. The <i>N</i>-Methyl-2-pyrrolidinone solvent was used to get a uniformly mixed slurry which was then spread on the Cu foil and dried in an oven at 80 °C. The cells were fabricated with NaVO<sub>3</sub> as a working electrode, glass fiber as a separator, and sodium metallic foil as a counter electrode. NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1 M) dissolved in an equal weight ratio of ethylene carbonate, diethyl carbonate, and propylene carbonate was used as the electrolyte. Cyclic voltammograms (CVs) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were conducted using a BioLogic potentiostat instrument (VMP3 BioLab, Inc.). The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was measured using a Maccor instrument by applying the repeated current pulses for 10 min at a current density of 11 mA g<sup>–1</sup> followed by relaxation for 60 min. The relaxation time was set longer to achieve the equilibrium potential for the given electrode. GITT measurements were taken over a complete galvanostatic cycle.</div><div class="NLM_p">In situ XRD was conducted using a laboratory-made coin cell with a 3 mm hole in the disk and lower and upper caps, and the cell was cycled using a multichannel WonATech (WBCS3000K8) instrument. XRD measurements were taken using the X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku R-AXIS IV++) at the Korea Institute of Science and Technology (KIST). The diffractometer is equipped with Mo Kα radiations with a wavelength of 0.7107 Å, and the data are recorded on an image plate with an exposure time of 300 s. After processing the data from the 2D image to the 1D XRD pattern, the 2θ values were changed from Mo Kα to Cu Kα radiations to compare the data with the standard.</div><div class="NLM_p last">For ex situ analysis, the samples were prepared by disassembling the cells at various dis/charged voltages. The electrodes were immersed in dimethyl carbonate solution to remove any surface salt contents. XANES measurements of V K-edge (5465 eV) were performed at 1D KIST, 7D XAFS, and 8C Nano XAFS beamlines at Pohang Light Sources (PLS-II). The energy was selected using a Si(111) double-crystal monochromator, the signals were detuned to ∼40% of the maximum intensity in the case of 1D and 7D beamlines, and a pure vanadium foil was used as a reference to calibrate the spectra. All the XANES measurements were performed in vacuum and the XANES spectra of electrodes were recorded in the transmission mode. The resulting spectra were analyzed using the ATHENA package. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) data were extracted by using the Hanning window function with <i>k</i><sup>2</sup> weighted in the <i>k</i>-ranges of 2.5–11 Å<sup>–1</sup>. It is worthy to mention here that the plotted data are converted without the phase corrected, so there is a shift (∼0.5 nm) in the observed bond lengths.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The structure of the as-synthesized material is determined using XRD as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. All the XRD peaks and lattice parameters of NaVO<sub>3</sub> are found to match with PDF card no. 01-072-2272, and the main peaks are indexed for clarity. NaVO<sub>3</sub> crystallizes in the monoclinic <i>C</i>12/<i>C</i>1 space group, and the lattice parameters are calculated to be <i>a</i> = 10.5692 Å, <i>b</i> = 9.4973 Å, <i>c</i> = 5.8772 Å, β = 108.35,° and <i>V</i> = 559.939 Å<sup>3</sup>. The material was found to have a high peak (220) over other planes which is presumably due to the preferred orientation of the crystals. The crystallite size was calculated using the Scherrer formula from three major peaks, that is, (200), (220), and (310), and its value was calculated to be 49 nm. The crystal structure of NaVO<sub>3</sub> along the <i>b</i>-axis is shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> where vanadium is tetrahedrally bonded with oxygen, and sodium ions are located at the interstitial sites between the V–O tetrahedral layers which are separated by a distance of 4.6763 Å. The large interlayer spacing could be useful for the sodium insertion/extraction process. The crystal structure of NaVO<sub>3</sub> consists of alternate channels where VO<sub>3</sub> chains are connected via oxygen at the corner to form the basic structural unit of VO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Powder XRD pattern of NaVO<sub>3</sub> where the diffraction peaks are indexed with the respective <i>hkl</i> values. The inset shows the crystal structure of NaVO<sub>3</sub> along the <i>b</i>-axis.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> powder is observed using SEM as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a where most of the particles are grown with a rod-like morphology. The length and width of these rods are in the range of 1–4 μm and 100–700 nm, respectively. Some agglomerated particles were also observed with random shapes. TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) show the scattered NaVO<sub>3</sub> particles with different shapes and sizes. To clearly observe the morphology, a single rod-like particle with a submicron dimension is shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) SEM image of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> powder. (b) TEM image of NaVO<sub>3</sub> and the inset shows the single rod-like morphology.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Further, the chemical composition of NaVO<sub>3</sub> was analyzed using XPS to study the electronic configuration and the survey spectrum is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. Prominent peaks of V 2p, O 1s, and Na 1s orbitals were detected in the XPS survey spectra and are plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b–d. The peaks at 517.1 and 524.5 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) correspond to the binding energies of the V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, and their energy positions are indicative of the V<sup>5+</sup> oxidation state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The peaks were deconvoluted to get insight into the V 2p bond, and three different peaks at 517.1 (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>), 523.6 (V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>), and 524.9 eV (V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) were observed, which correspond to V–O bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The deconvolution of the Na 1s peak (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) at energies of 1071.2 and 1072 eV represents the different interactions of sodium with alternate bonds in the structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The deconvolution of the O 1s peak (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) shows two peaks at 530.2 and 530.7 eV, which are typical characteristic peaks of metal–oxygen bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Thus, XRD and XPS confirm the successful formation of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> compound.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XPS survey of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> powder where prominent peaks from V 2p, O 1s, and Na 1s are marked at their respective energies. (b) V 2p, (c) Na 1s, and (d) O 1s peaks are XPS spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrodes were measured using the half-cell. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the CV for the three cycles measured in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. In the first cycle, two distinct peaks at 1.2 and 0.2 V appear during reduction, presumably attributed to the irreversible reactions at the surface of the electrode with the electrolyte and formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer, respectively. A pair of sharp redox peaks was observed at ∼0.1 V and could be related to the insertion/extraction of sodium in the NaVO<sub>3</sub>. The second and third cycles almost overlap, demonstrating the reversibility of the material. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows the galvanostatic dis/charge profile of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode at a current density of 9 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. The material exhibits a high discharge capacity of 359 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle with a sloping profile. A charge capacity of 196 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was recorded in the first cycle which corresponds to a Coulombic efficiency of 55%. The low value of Coulombic efficiency belongs to the formation of the SEI layer and its value improved in the subsequent cycles. The second cycle delivers discharge and charge capacities of 223 and 196 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which corresponds to an improved Coulombic efficiency of 88%. The specific capacity decreases continuously in the subsequent cycles as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. However, the Coulombic efficiency was improved, and the electrode exhibits a charge capacity of 125 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the 80th cycle with a Coulombic efficiency of &gt;99%. The rate capability of NaVO<sub>3</sub> was measured at various current densities in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. The electrode delivered charge capacities of 164, 137, 93, and 51 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 11, 22, 110, and 220 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode during the first, second, and third cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Charge–discharge profile of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode at the first and second cycles at a current density of 9 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycle test at a current density of 9 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. (d) Rate capability test in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at the current densities of 11, 22, 110, and 220 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrode reaction insights and sodium diffusivity in the layered NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode are investigated using the CV, GITT, and EIS techniques. CV provides the information of the measurement of the current flowing through the electrode material as a function of the externally controlled potential. These current signals provide detailed information about the reaction associated with electron-transfer steps. The sodium diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) is calculated from CV measurements by assuming that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the solid phase is a semi-infinite or finite diffusion process where the peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) is proportional to the square root of the scan rate (<i>v</i>) and it follows the Nernstian equation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the CV curves of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode measured at scan rates of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, and 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. In the first cathodic scan at a rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the peak at ∼0.7 V could be attributed to the formation of the SEI layer. The anodic scan shows two broad peaks at 0.3 and 2.4 V, which could be related to the oxidation of vanadium. The peak current and gap between cathodic and anodic scans increase with increasing the scan rates, which indicates an increased polarization due to kinetic limitations related to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> particles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows the relationship graph between <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> and the square root of <i>v</i>. The linear relationship between the two indicates that the sodium diffusion in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode is a rate-determining step. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> can be calculated by Cottrell <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>A</i> represents the area between the electrode and the electrolyte, <i>C</i><sub>Na</sub> is the bulk concentration of the Na<sup>+</sup> ions and its values are determined by assuming the concentration of sodium in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode, and <i>n</i> is the number of charge transfer, which depends on the cathodic respective anodic reaction. By substituting all these values in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> is calculated to be 2.7935 × 10<sup>–16</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. The calculated value of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> for NaVO<sub>3</sub> is lower than that of the previously reported layered oxide anode where <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> is calculated to be 7.9 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> in the case of the layered Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The lower values of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> in NaVO<sub>3</sub> particles could be the reason for less specific capacities at higher current densities as the electrode faces more polarization in large-sized particles because of the relatively higher time constant for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CVs of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode at various scan rates and (b) peak current as a function of the square root of the scan rate. (c) GITT curve plotted with sodium diffusion coefficient in NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (d) linearly fitted potential response as a function of the square root of time. (e) EIS graphs of the as-fabricated and after the 80th cycle of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode, and the inset shows the equivalent circuit. (f) Real parts of complex impedance plotted as a function of ω<sup>–1/2</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, to understand the kinetic performance in NaVO<sub>3</sub>, <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> is calculated using GITT measurements during the sodium insertion/extraction process. GITT was developed by Weppner and Huggins to measure the diffusion coefficient based on chronopotentiometry in a mixed conductor by assuming the diffusion process is one-dimensional in a solid solution. GITT also combines both the steady-state and transient measurements as well as thermodynamic data. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> A constant current flux is applied to the host electrode materials to insert/extract the sodium ions for a limited time period, and this process changes the overall composition and cell potential. Subsequently, the change in the cell potential is recorded for each current pulse and the diffusion coefficient is determined. The GITT discharge/charge profile of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c in the potential range of 0.01–3.0 V. During consecutive current pulses applied to the electrode, the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into NaVO<sub>3</sub> influences the electrode potential, and the resulting potential response is calculated to be a linear function of the square root of pulse time (√τ) as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. The slope and a linear fit <i>R</i><sup>2</sup> value of the fitted line are calculated to be −0.106 and 0.982, respectively. <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> can be calculated using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> by supposing that sodium transport follows Fick’s law.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where τ is the current pulse time, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>) is the molar volume of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> (g) is the active material weight, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> (g mol<sup>–1</sup>) is the molecular weight, and <i>S</i> (cm<sup>2</sup>) is the electrode surface area. Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> are measured for each step and denote the change in the steady-state voltage at a plateau and the total change in cell voltage during pulse time, respectively. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values are calculated for each pulse by measuring and substituting the above parameters in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> value is calculated to be 1.15715 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the start of the discharge process; it decreases with the increase in the insertion of sodium ions into the electrode material, and the value is calculated to be 2.02546 × 10<sup>–17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the last step of the discharge process. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> value is calculated to be 5.66377 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the first step of the charge process; it also shows a decreasing trend with the sodium extraction process from the electrode material, and the value is calculated to be 2.25694 × 10<sup>–16</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the end of the charge process. The electrode material has shown low values of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> toward the discharging and charging depths, which indicates that sodium ions face high polarization at the end of each process. This phenomenon could be ascribed to the fact that the electrochemical reactions in these regions are severely sluggish accompanied by the strong interactions of the sodium ions with the other surrounding ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To further probe the sodium diffusivity before and after cycling in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode, EIS was conducted in a sodium half-cell at open circuit potential. EIS is a powerful tool to follow the kinetics of sodium insertion/extraction into/from the electrode material where a collective response of kinetic processes is measured as a cell impedance. Further, the collective response consisting of interfacial and diffusion processes can be separated on the frequency scale. Moreover, EIS also offers an easy way to identify the side reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The Nyquist plots of freshly prepared and after 80th cycle cells are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. An equivalent circuit is used to fit the curves using the ZView software, and the circuit diagram is shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. The EIS curves are composed of semicircles at a high-frequency range which are related to the charge-transfer resistance at the electrolyte–electrode interface and a straight sloping line at the low-frequency range, corresponding to the sodium diffusion into the active material. The circuit is composed of Ohmic resistance of the electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at the interface, a constant phase element of the double layer, Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>W</sub>) which is related to the diffusion process, and a double layer capacitance (<i>C</i>). The as-fabricated layered NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode shows a low <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of 27 Ω, indicating better kinetic activity, whereas the electrode shows a high <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of 153 Ω after the 80th cycle. Moreover, sodium ion transport inside the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode was also calculated using the EIS spectra. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> was calculated from the sloping line in the Nyquist plot, according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>where <i>T</i> represents the absolute temperature (295.15 K), <i>R</i> represents the gas constant (8.314 J mol<sup>–1</sup> K<sup>–1</sup>), <i>A</i> denotes the surface area (1.13 cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>C</i> represents the concentration of sodium ions calculated from the ratio of the tapped density and molecular weight, <i>F</i> indicates Faraday’s constant (96486 C mol<sup>–1</sup>), <i>n</i> represents the number of electrons associated with the reaction, and σ is the Warburg factor calculated based on the relation between ω<sup>–1/2</sup> and <i>Z</i><sub>real</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f). The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> was calculated to be 1.368 × 10<sup>–15</sup> and 4.952 × 10<sup>–16</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for the as-fabricated and after the 80th cycle, respectively. Overall, sodium transport was measured to be lower after cycling mainly due to increased polarization and structural changes.</div><div class="NLM_p">The reaction mechanism of the electrode material has been explored using in situ XRD, ex situ TEM, and XANES. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows the charge/discharge profile (left) and their corresponding XRD patterns (right). The ball-milling of the prepared material prior to making electrodes results in the lattice disorder and mismatch in the relative intensity of the XRD peaks, which can be observed in the in situ XRD of the electrode material. Ball-milling is a well-known technique to change the particle size, surface area, microstructure, and so forth of solid materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The cell diffraction and Cu peaks were eliminated to clarify the diffraction pattern from the electrode material. As the sodiation process starts, a significant gradual reduction in the intensity of all the diffraction peaks is observed. This behavior of decrease in the peak intensity is associated with the sloping trend of the discharge profile, which leads to the amorphous-like structure. The peak intensities continuously decrease during the sodium extraction process, suggesting the amorphous-like phase of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> anode at the end of the first cycle. For a better understanding of the microcrystalline structure, we have conducted ex situ TEM. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a displays the HR-TEM image of the pristine material where it shows good crystallinity, and the lattice fringes corresponding to <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.519 and 0.687 nm are observed which belongs to (110) and (200) planes, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b shows the HR-TEM image of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> anode at a fully discharged state to 0.01 V. The image reveals an amorphous-like microstructure with no visible fringes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c shows the HR-TEM image of the fully charged sample to 3.0 V which again shows an amorphous-like structure. Overall, the HR-TEM results show that the material transforms into the amorphous-like structure during the first discharge process, and once it loses its crystallinity, it remains in an amorphous-like phase. It is difficult to extract the structural and Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction mechanism information from the amorphous phase, but the material shows high capacity and stable cycle performance. However, the amorphous-type anodes have been previously reported; they possess the advantage of acting as a buffer to relieve the strains upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and result in improved cycle performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Ex situ XANES was measured at the points denoted on the dis/charge profile as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d, and the resulting XANES spectra along with V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>5+</sup> standards are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e. The pristine sample shows a relatively high preedge peak which is caused because of tetrahedral symmetry as already discussed in the XRD results. When the electrode is discharged to point 2, there is not much shift in both the preedge and main peaks. However, discharging to points 3 and 4, the spectra show a substantial shift to a lower energy, indicating a reduction in the oxidation state from V<sup>5+</sup> to V<sup>4+</sup>. The charge process (points 5 and 6) during the first cycle shows oxidation of vanadium; however, the spectrum at point 6 is at a lower energy than the pristine sample (point 1), which might be related to the partial irreversibility or structural changes in the material. A fully charged spectrum during the second cycle (point 7) is found to overlap the fully charged spectrum (point 6), which suggests the reversibility of the material in subsequent cycles during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Overall, our results suggest that the electrochemical reaction in the NaVO<sub>3</sub> anode takes place between V<sup>4+</sup> ↔ V<sup>5+</sup> oxidation states with high reversibility after the first cycle. EXAFS was performed to get insights into the local structure. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>f shows the EXAFS spectra of the pristine, fully discharged, and recharged electrodes. The dominant peak at ∼1.2 nm corresponds to the nearest V–O interaction, and the next peaks in a range of 2.9–3.4 nm belong to the V–Na interaction for the pristine NaVO<sub>3</sub> spectrum. The inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>f shows the bond distance between the V–O and V–Na interactions. The different bond lengths of the V–O interaction in tetrahedral coordination suggest the distorted symmetry, which was also observed in the form of a high preedge peak in the XANES spectra. The presence of the V–O interaction peak in the EXAFS spectra suggests the preservation of the original structure of NaVO<sub>3</sub>. However, the slight shift and difference in the intensity of the V–O interaction peaks in the fully discharged and recharged spectra occurred, which is due to the Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Charge–discharge profile (left) during in situ XRD measurement and the corresponding XRD patterns (right).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/medium/am-2018-03571d_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Ex situ TEM of a (a) pristine, (b) fully discharged, and (c) fully charged NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode. (d) Charge–discharge profile of the NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode where marked points indicate where the XANES spectra are measured and (e) the resulting XANES spectra along with VO<sub>2</sub> and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> standards. (f) EXAFS spectra of pristine (1), fully discharged (4), fully recharged (6), and fully recharged during the second cycle (7). The inset shows the local structure coordination with the nearest neighboring atoms.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03571/asset/images/large/am-2018-03571d_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03571&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the single-phase layered NaVO<sub>3</sub> has been obtained with the solid-state method along the (220) orientation. The NaVO<sub>3</sub> electrode shows promising electrochemical properties where it delivered a high specific capacity of 196 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle and 125 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the 80th cycle with a high Coulombic efficiency of &gt;99%. The kinetic study was systematically conducted using different techniques such as CV, GITT, and EIS to calculate the sodium diffusivity. GITT is applied to the discharge/charge process, and <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> is calculated at each pulse where it shows a large variation in the values. <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> is calculated to be 1.15715 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the start and 2.02546 × 10<sup>–17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the end of the discharge process, whereas its value is recorded as 5.66377 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the beginning and 2.25694 × 10<sup>–16</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the end of the charge process. Sodium ions face higher polarization toward the end of each process. The reaction mechanism is investigated, which suggests that NaVO<sub>3</sub> transformed to the amorphous-like structure as confirmed by ex situ TEM but retained high reversibility after the second cycle as demonstrated by the XANES analysis.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b03571" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Author</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Kyung Yoon Chung</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">Center

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Radially aligned hierarchical columnar structure as a cathode material for high energy density sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Oh, Seung-Min; Myung, Seung-Taek; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Belharouak, Ilias; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries operating at ambient temp. hold great promise for use in grid energy storage owing to their significant cost advantages.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NASICON-Structured NaTi2(PO4)3@C Nanocomposite as the Low Operation-Voltage Anode Material for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Dongxue; Liu, Qiang; Chen, Chaoji; Li, Malin; Meng, Xing; Bie, Xiaofei; Wei, Yingjin; Huang, Yunhui; Du, Fei; Wang, Chunzhong; Chen, Gang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na3V2(PO4)3/C composite as the intercalation-type anode material for sodium-ion batteries with superior rate capability and long-cycle life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Dongxue; Chen, Nan; Li, Malin; Wang, Chunzhong; Ehrenberg, Helmut; Bie, Xiaofei; Wei, Yingjin; Chen, Gang; Du, Fei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A Na3V2(PO4)3/C (NVP/C) composite is successfully synthesized by sol-gel method and examd. as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries by means of galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles, cyclic voltammetry, rate performance and cyclic voltammetry comprehensively.  The NVP/C electrode delivers a reversible capacity of about 170 mA-h/g between 3.0 and 0.01 V at a c.d. of 20 mA/g corresponding to three sodium ions insertion/extn. processes.  Besides the voltage plateau at 1.57 V, another novel working platform at around 0.28 V is found for the first time in both charging and discharging profiles, possibly owing to the further redn. of vanadium.  NVP/C exhibits an excellent rate capability and long-cycle stability with a capacity retention of 62% after 3000 cycles at a high charge rate of 10C (2 A/g).  Moreover, the intercalation-type Na-ions storage mechanism is proposed on the basis of ex situ x-ray diffraction and high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy.  Our findings reveal that the NVP/C sample is a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries due to its superior rate capability and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kajiyama, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iwatate, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishikawa, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hitomi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okuyama, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Usui, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kajiyama, Masataka; Iwatate, Junichi; Nishikawa, Heisuke; Hitomi, Shuji; Okuyama, Ryoichi; Usui, Ryo; Yamada, Yasuhiro; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">512-517</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.  However, their reversible capacity is typically limited to 100 mA-h/g.  Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.  These results will contribute to the development of rechargeable batteries from the earth-abundant elements operable at room temp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.01.080"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A combination of operando X-ray diffraction, pair distribution function (PDF) anal. coupled with electrochem. measurements and M.ovrddot.ossbauer spectroscopy elucidates the nature of the phase transitions induced by insertion and extn. of sodium ions in P2-Na0.67[NiyMn0.5+yFe0.5-2y]O2 (y = 0, 0.10, 0.15).  When phase transitions are avoided, the optimal cathode material - P2-Na0.67Fe0.2Mn0.65Ni0.15O2 - delivers 25% more energy than the unsubstituted material, sustaining high specific energy (350 Wh kg-1) at moderate rates and maintains 80% of the original energy d. after 150 cycles - a significant improvement in performance vs. the unsubstituted analog.  The crystal structure of the high voltage phase is solved for the first time by X-ray PDF anal. of P2-Na0.67-zFe0.5Mn0.5O2 (where z ∼ 0.5), revealing that migration of the transition metals - particularly Fe3+ - into tetrahedral sites in the interlayer space occurs at high potential.  This results in new short range order between two adjacent layers.  Although the transition metal migration is reversible as proven by electrochem. performance, it induces a large disfavorable cell polarization.  The deleterious high voltage transition is mitigated by substitution of Fe3+ by Mn4+/Ni2+, giving rise to better cycling performance.  Moreover, as demonstrated by 57Fe M.ovrddot.ossbauer spectroscopy, the much lower ratio of Fe4+O6 to Fe3+O6 obsd. systematically across the range of Ni content - compared to the values expected from a purely ionic model - suggests redox activity involves the O-2p orbitals owing to their overlap with the transition metal-3d orbitals.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1038/srep40910"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mehmood, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ha, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the feasibility of using reduced graphene oxide (RGO) as a cost-effective and high performance cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Graphene oxide is synthesized by a modified Hummers' method and reduced using a solid-state microwave irradn. method.  The RGO electrode delivers an exceptionally stable discharge capacity of 240 mAh g-1 with a stable long cycling up to 1000 cycles.     <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15422-15429</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The surface of olivine NaFePO4 was modified with polythiophene (PTh) to develop a high-performance cathode material for use in Na-ion batteries.  The Rietveld refinement results of the prepd. material reveal that PTh-coated NaFePO4 belongs to a space group of Pnma with lattice parameters of a = 10.40656 Å, b = 6.22821 Å, and c = 4.94971 Å.  Uncoated NaFePO4 delivers a discharge capacity of 108 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 10 mA g-1 within a voltage range of 2.2-4.0 V.  Conversely, the PTh-coated NaFePO4 electrode exhibits significantly improved electrochem. performance, where it exhibits a discharge capacity of 142 mAh g-1 and a stable cycle life over 100 cycles, with a capacity retention of 94%.  The NaFePO4/PTh electrode also exhibits satisfactory performance at high current densities, and reversible capacities of 70 mAh g-1 at 150 mA g-1 and 42 mAh g-1 at 300 mA g-1 are obtained compared with negligible capacities without coating.  The related electrochem. reaction mechanism has been investigated using in situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), which revealed a systematic change of Fe valence and reversible contraction/expansion of Fe-O octahedra upon desodiation/sodiation.  The ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) results suggest that the deintercalation in NaFePO4/PTh electrodes proceeds through a stable intermediate phase and the lattice parameters show a reversible contraction/expansion of unit cell during cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. techniques have been used to study the reversible insertion of sodium into hard-carbon host structures at room temp.  In this paper we compare these results with those for lithium insertion in the same materials and demonstrate the presence of similar alkali metal insertion mechanisms in both cases.  Despite the gravimetric capacities being lower for sodium than lithium insertion, we have achieved a reversible sodium capacity of 300 mAh/g, close to that for lithium insertion in graphitic carbon anode materials.  Such materials may therefore be useful as anodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Murata, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishikawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozeki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogata, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujiwara, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1149/1.2221153"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Doeff, M.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. insertion of sodium ions into carbon using solid polymer electrolytes or org. liq. electrolytes is described.  Cells with the configuration Na/P(EO)6NaCF3SO3/CP(EO) (PEO = polyethylene oxide) or Na/liq. electrolyte/C were galvanostatically discharged, charged, and cycled.  The extent of insertion into C (i.e., x in Na+Cax-) was found to be a strong function of the type and particle size of the carbon used, and the reversibility of the process was highly dependent upon the type of electrolyte used.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct atomic-scale confirmation of three-phase storage mechanism in Li4Ti5O12 anodes for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun Yang; Zhao Liang; Pan Huilin; Lu Xia; Gu Lin; Hu Yong-Sheng; Li Hong; Armand Michel; Ikuhara Yuichi; Chen Liquan; Huang Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1870</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries attract increasing attention for large-scale energy storage applications in renewable energy and smart grid.  However, the development of suitable anode materials remains a challenging issue.  Here we demonstrate that the spinel Li4Ti5O12, well-known as a 'zero-strain' anode for lithium-ion batteries, can also store sodium, displaying an average storage voltage of 0.91 V.  With an appropriate binder, the Li4Ti5O12 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 155 mAh g(-1) and presents the best cyclability among all reported oxide-based anode materials.  Density functional theory calculations predict a three-phase separation mechanism, 2Li4Ti5O12+6Na(+)+6e(-)↔Li7Ti5O12+Na6LiTi5O12, which has been confirmed through in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction and advanced scanning transmission electron microscope imaging techniques.  The three-phase separation reaction has never been seen in any insertion electrode materials for lithium- or sodium-ion batteries.  Furthermore, interfacial structure is clearly resolved at an atomic scale in electrochemically sodiated Li4Ti5O12 for the first time via the advanced electron microscopy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/cm202076g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Senguttuvan, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rousse, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seznec, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacín, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The discharge properties of a Na/Ni3S2 cell using 1 M NaCF3SO3 in tetra(ethylene glycol)dimethyl ether liq. electrolyte were investigated at room temp.  The products were characterized by X-ray diffraction, SEM and energy dispersive spectroscopy.  Electrochem. properties of Na/Ni3S2 cells were also presented by cyclic voltammetry and the galvanostatic current method.  Na/Ni3S2 cells have an initial discharge capacity of 420 mAh g-1 with a plateau potential at 0.94 V vs. Na/Na+.         </div><div class="casAbstract">While much research effort was devoted to the development of advanced Li-ion batteries for renewal energy storage applications, the Na-ion battery is also of considerable interest because Na is one of the most abundant elements in the Earth's crust.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of the Na Intercalation Mechanism into Nanosized V2O5/C Composite Cathode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ali, Ghulam; Lee, Ji-Hoon; Oh, Si Hyoung; Cho, Byung Won; Nam, Kyung-Wan; Chung, Kyung Yoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6032-6039</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">There is a significant interest to develop high-performance and cost-effective electrode materials for next-generation sodium ion batteries.  Herein, we report a facile synthesis method for nanosized V2O5/C composite cathodes and their electrochem. performance as well as energy storage mechanism.  The composite exhibits a discharge capacity of 255 mAh/g at a c.d. of 0.05 C, which surpasses that of previously reported layered oxide materials.  Furthermore, the electrode shows good rate capability, discharge capacity of 160 mAh/g at a c.d. of 1 C.  The reaction mechanism of V2O5 upon sodium insertion/extn. is investigated using ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and synchrotron based near edge X-ray absorption fine structure (NEXAFS) spectroscopy.  Ex situ XRD result of the fully discharged state reveals the appearance of NaV2O5 as a major phase with minor Na2V2O5 phase.  Upon insertion of sodium into the array of parallel ladders of V2O5, it was confirmed that lattice parameter of c is increased by 9.09%, corresponding to the increase in the unit-cell vol. of 9.2%.  NEXAFS results suggest that the charge compensation during de/sodiation process accompanied by the reversible changes in the oxidn. state of vanadium (V4+ ↔ V5+).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b02480"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pei, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">VO2 Nanoflakes as the Cathode Material of Hybrid Magnesium–Lithium-Ion Batteries with High Energy Density</span>. <i>ACS Appl. However, just like other Li-V-O compds., the application of the LiVO3 cathode is restricted by its poor cycling stability and rate capability.  In this paper, we report nanoplate-stacked baguette-like LiVO3 by an in situ template method.  In this unique structure, the microsized baguette shape can help to inhibit the dissoln. of vanadium in comparison with nanosized LiVO3, and the stacked nanoplates can provide short diffusion pathways for lithium ions.  Benefiting from these advantages, this material can deliver a very high capacity of 275.6 mA-h/g with excellent cycling stability.  Moreover, a capacity of 156.1 mA-h/g can be obtained up to 4C (1200 mA/g).  The exceptional electrochem. performance suggests the use of nanoplate-stacked baguette-like LiVO3 as an alternative cathode material for high-energy lithium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical nanostructures have been demonstrated as one of the most ideal electrode materials in energy storage systems owing to the synergistic combination of the advantages of both nanostructures and microstructures.  In this work, 3D V6O13 nanotextiles built from interconnected one-dimensional nanogrooves with diam. of 20-50 nm were fabricated via a facile soln.-redox-based self-assembly route at room temp., and the mesh size in the textile structure can be controllably tuned by adjusting the precursor concn.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Alkaline earth metal vanadates as sodium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu Xiaoming; Niu Chaojiang; Wang Xuanpeng; Huang Lei; Wang Junhui; Pu Liting; Ren Wenhao; Shi Changwei; Meng Jiasheng; Mai Liqiang; Duan Manyi; Song Bo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The abundance of sodium resources indicates the potential of sodium-ion batteries as emerging energy storage devices.  However, the practical application of sodium-ion batteries is hindered by the limited electrochemical performance of electrode materials, especially at the anode side.  Here, we identify alkaline earth metal vanadates as promising anodes for sodium-ion batteries.  The prepared calcium vanadate nanowires possess intrinsically high electronic conductivity (&gt; 100 S cm(-1)), small volume change (&lt; 10%), and a self-preserving effect, which results in a superior cycling and rate performance and an applicable reversible capacity (&gt; 300 mAh g(-1)), with an average voltage of ∼1.0 V.  The specific sodium-storage mechanism, beyond the conventional intercalation or conversion reaction, is demonstrated through in situ and ex situ characterizations and theoretical calculations.  This work explores alkaline earth metal vanadates for sodium-ion battery anodes and may open a direction for energy storage.The development of suitable anode materials is essential to advance sodium-ion battery technologies.  Here the authors report that alkaline earth metal vanadates are promising candidates due to the favorable electrochemical properties and interesting sodium-storage mechanism.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1021/cm502268u"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chae, O.
